==PAGE:5==
 
i 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
Abstract 
 
Several studies have shown improved oil recovery in core plugs and wells by stimulation with ultrasonic 
elastic waves. Such negative skin effect may not last for long time and be limited to the near wellbore 
area, however the recovery mechanisms are not well defined or understood. One possible mechanism 
could be associated with the out-of-phase movement (inertial forces dominates the viscous forces in the 
fluid bulk (Fabricius & Andreassen, 2010)) between solid frame and saturating pore fluid induced in 
the sample, provided that the frequency of the elastic wave exceeds Biot's critical frequency. Biot’s 
critical frequency (fc) as a reference frequency value depends on the fluid/pore space characteristics, 
that defines a limit between low- and high-frequency regimes under the applied elastic wave (Fabricius 
& Andreassen, 2010). It acts like a friction threshold factor between fluid and solid grains of the rock 
frame. In this study, critical frequency is characterized after petrophysical characterization (N2 Poro-
perm, NMR, BET and MICP) of 4 Copenhagen limestone samples of different pore sizes. Acoustic 
velocity of the samples (dry and tape water saturated) under multi-stage isotropic triaxial compaction 
are measured and sonic waves first arrivals are picked up and assessed using the Iso-frame model for 
effective modelling of elastic moduli (Fabricius, Høier, Japsen, & Korsbech, 2007) (Fabricius, Bächle, 
& Eberli, 2010). Then, the frequency regime for each sample was assessed relative to an emitted p-
wave frequency of 210 kHz. However, all samples are in the low frequency regime, validation of 
Gassmann's fluid substitution showed underpredictions for two less permeable samples and the other 2 
samples were nearly matching the measured bulk modulus at different triaxial load steps. It was shown 
that this could probably owe to the pore size distribution (PSD) and how much of the pores is larger 
than a characteristic threshold pore radius, that correspond to the Biot’s Critical Frequency (fc) of the 
examined sample. Samples’ PSD with higher contribution from such larger pores are more likely to 
experience the high frequency effects even with frequency ratios less than unity. Moreover, the IF 
values that reflect on grain-to-grain contacts and tortuosity, could possibly convey a reason for the poor 
pore communication, that induce uneven pore pressure gradient distribution violating Gassmann’s 
substitution assumptions.

1 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
1 
Introduction 
 
1.1 
Background 
An elastic wave as a mechanical disturbance propagates through an isotropic homogeneous composite 
material through elemental vibrations or an oscillating motion (FJÆR, M. HOLT, HORSRUD, & 
RAAEN, 2008). Each finite element of the material is pushed by the adjacent elements. This is 
mathematically governed by wave equation that is based on the newton’s second law of motion: Force 
= Mass × Acceleration. For a simple wave in the x-direction, the force is: 
 
Fx = ∂σx
∂x +
∂τxy
∂y + ∂τxz
∂z  
(1.1) 
where σx, τxy and τxz are the stresses related to a surface normal to the x-axis, representing normal 
stress and shear stress initiated by a y- and z-direction force, respectively (FJÆR, M. HOLT, 
HORSRUD, & RAAEN, 2008). 
Assuming no shear contribution (τxy and τxz are zeros), then equation (1.1) is reduced into: 
 
∂σx
∂x = ρB
∂2u
∂t2 
(1.2) 
 
The acceleration term gained by an elemental mass per unit volume in a plane is given by the material’s 
bulk density (ρB) and the second derivative of the displacement of the element (u) in x direction with 
respect to time. While the force applied on such element per unit volume is governed by the stress, 
which is related to material elasticity (stiffness) and strain in Hooke’s Law.  
Such elastic oscillations happen at certain repetitive patterns (frequency) at certain orientations, that can 
last for whatever time duration. The particles can either vibrate (oscillations) in parallel to the wave 
propagation (force direction) plane or normal to it. The difference is illustrated in Figure 1.1. 
1. For a parallel particles’ vibration/wave propagation phase, there is single wave phase that 
particles could accelerate parallel to, causing a temporary compressional elastic volume 
change, and this wave phase is named longitudinal (Compressional, Primary or P).  
2. The other normal particles’ oscillation/wave motion phase can possibly take place in two 
directions that are defined by the geometrical orientation of the particles’ oscillation. Therefore, 
there are two waves with two perpendicular polarization that are called Transverse (Shear, 
Secondary or S) that cause a medium elastic shape change, not a volume change.  
 
Figure 1.1: Particle oscillation's orientation in P- and S-waves propagation planes. (Courtesy of (FJÆR, M. HOLT, 
HORSRUD, & RAAEN, 2008)) 
How fast those waves travel (particles’ acceleration) depends on the material’s stiffness in the direction 
where particles tend to accelerate. And for a wave penetrating a solid medium, there are two main wave 
phases: P and S waves. P wave is a single wave, while the S wave has two possible polarizations, giving 
a rise to S1 and S2 waves, that are perpendicular to each other (i.e., one oscillation is as indicated in 
lower section of Figure 1.1, and the other is perpendicular to the page).  
==PAGE:10==
 
 
2 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
The travelling velocity of those phases is a direct indication of medium stiffness, conveying how much 
the medium will resist compression and shear strain under uniaxial compression (P-wave) and Shear 
stress (S-wave). Phase velocities are obtained by solving the differential wave equations for both 
parallel (P) and normal (S) phases, and they are correlated to the corresponding elastic modulus and 
medium bulk density as following: 
 
Vp = √M
ρB
= √K + 4
3 G
ρB
 
(1.3) 
and 
 
Vs = √G
ρB
 
(1.4) 
where Vp and Vs are the P- and S-wave phase velocities in [m/s]. M, G1 and K are the compressional 
(uni-axial or P-Wave), shear and bulk elastic moduli of the medium in [GPa]. ΡB is the bulk density of 
the medium in [gm/cc]. 
When the medium is porous and a certain compressible fluid is introduced into pore volume, such 
composite elastic medium (Johnson, Koplik, & Dashen, 1987) will significantly deviate from a solid 
homogenous case; in terms of the elastic stiffness defined by equations (1.3) and (1.4). 
The ultrasonic velocities are experimentally measured by picking up the net2 first wave arrival time. 
And the bulk density of the composite material ρB will be given in terms of the porosity fraction and 
the pure density of individual constituents; pore fluid (ρf) and solid (ρgrain): 
 
ρB = ∅ρf + (1 −∅)ρgrain 
(1.5) 
 
Accordingly, stiffness or resistance to strain under stress of the porous medium is sensitive to the 
introduced fluid fraction in the pores. The pore pressure counteracts the external applied stress on the 
bulk sample, provided that the pore fluid (basically liquids) has a significant elastic modulus. Moreover, 
for a propagating elastic wave fluid particles’ oscillation can possibly play a role as well in the bulk 
elastic behavior of the porous rock. If the oscillations yield frictional forces due to an out-of-phase 
relative motion between fluid and solid grains trapping those oscillations, such friction will also 
positively reflect on the composite rock strength. This is the bottom line of the study, whose objective 
is to experimentally investigate the pore fluid particles’ oscillations sensitivity to the applied elastic 
wave frequency and how this ultimately affects the limestone core samples stiffness under different 
compressional stress levels. And the elastic waves’ velocity sensitivity is the quantitative approach for 
that, supported by some other physical theories and definitions governing the physics of elastic wave 
propagation in porous media. 
 
1.2 
Physical Definitions and Theories 
 
1.2.1 
Biot’s Critical (Reference) Frequency 
Biot’s theory of elastic wave propagation considers the compressible fluid fraction and its particles’ 
displacement under the wave propagation. The theory distinguished between two scenarios according 
 
 
1 Some literatures use H and µ for the compressional and shear elastic moduli. 
2 The travelling time only in the rock core sample, after subtracting the lag time in circuits. 
==PAGE:11==
 
 
3 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
to the frequency of the stress wave and the induced oscillations repetitive pattern (Biot, 1956) (Biot, 
1956): 
1. 
Low Frequency Range: the fluid particles and solid grains could be in a dynamic compatibility 
without relative motion between fluid and solid. With such low frequency, the wavelength is 
high, relative to the finite element size in the pore fluid bulk. Therefore, the fluid motion takes 
place in-phased with the motion of the solid grains. The fluid motion or local pore flow is of 
the Poiseuille laminar flow (hydraulic flow in a pipe with certain inner radius), where there is 
a dominance of viscous forces. 
2. 
High Frequency Range: beyond a certain reference or characteristic frequency value, the 
inertial forces dominate the viscous forces in the pore fluid. The saturation fluid oscillations 
become out-of-phase with the solid grains motion, which results in significant friction at the 
solid/fluid interface. 
And the characteristics reference (critical) Biot’s frequency is defined by: 
 
fc = ∅
k
η
2πρf
 
(1.6) 
which is a composite relation between the pore channel radius (represented in porosity ∅ and 
permeability k in [mD]) and kinematic fluid viscosity3 (which is dynamic viscosity 𝜂 in [c. poise] over 
fluid density).  
Therefore, Biot’s critical frequency is an analogue to Reynolds number for fluid flow (Fabricius & 
Andreassen, 2010). The Reynolds number distinguish between laminar and turbulent regimes. 
Similarly, Biot’s critical frequency for a certain pore fluid and pore size marks the transition limit 
between viscous forces (in-phase pore fluid/solid grains motion) and inertial forces predominance (out-
of-phase relative fluid/solid motion) when the fluid/solid friction will be tangible.  
The out-of-phase relative fluid/solid motion implies that the wave velocity will be changed and sensitive 
to the frequency, which is a phenomenon defined as Velocity Dispersion (FJÆR, M. HOLT, 
HORSRUD, & RAAEN, 2008). Also, the wave energy will attenuate faster with higher critical 
frequency characteristics as the induced friction drains more of the energy than if it was a low frequency 
and in-phase motion. Moreover, the high frequency regime could stiffen the solid frame (effective 
strength of the bulk porous rock) owing to the intertial friction at the fluid/solid interface, compared 
with the low frequency regime. 
Moreover, there is another distinction between the low and high frequency regimes introduced by David 
Johanson et al. called the Viscous Skin Depth (𝛿) (Johnson, Koplik, & Dashen, 1987): 
 
δ = √η
ρf
2
ω = √
η
πf ρf
 
(1.7) 
where η is the saturation fluid dynamic viscosity [c. poise], ρf is the fluid density in [gm/cc], ω is the 
angular frequency [rad/s] which related to the frequency f in [Hz].  
The physical meaning of the viscous skin depth is the distance 𝛿 from the pore wall towards the pore 
center where the elastic wave velocity is zero (Fabricius, Bächle, & Eberli, 2010). In other words, the 
skin depth to the solid surface is a reduction in the porosity and possibly reduces the permeability in 
turn, and the thickness depends on the ions’ concentration in the saturation fluid (electrolyte). The more 
 
 
3 Kinematic viscosity tells how fast the fluid is moving when a certain force (elastic wave stress) is applied, while 
dynamic viscosity gives how much force is needed to achieve a certain fluid flow rate. 
==PAGE:12==
 
 
4 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
ions are in the water4, the less the skin distance is, as explained by K. Andreassen et al. (Fabricius & 
Andreassen, 2010). 
Therefore, in the high frequency regime, the hydraulic radius of the pore space (which is defined later 
in Gassmann’s Substitution Validation, equation (4.5)) will be significantly large compared with the 
viscous skin depth. And the resistance to the propagation of the elastic wave is mainly from the out-of-
phase relative inertial (out-of-phase) motion of the fluid particles. However, for the low frequency 
regime, the hydraulic radius is small compared to the viscous skin depth and the wave propagation is 
resisted by the viscous friction of pore fluid, as explained by F. Ida et al. (Fabricius, Bächle, & Eberli, 
2010). 
 
1.2.2 
Gassmann’s Fluid Substitution 
The fluid substitution problem is a very interesting way to predict the saturated rock wave velocities 
based on available dry rock velocities or different fluid saturations. Generally, when a porous rock is 
compressed and the pore volume is saturated with a compressible liquid, any propagating elastic wave 
will induce a pore-pressure change. This pressure resists the compression over the rock and therefore 
gives the saturated (wet) rock more stiffness (higher bulk modulus), compared with another case with 
air as a saturation fluid in a dry rock. Accordingly, Gassmann theory predicts such resulting increase in 
the effective bulk modulus of the saturated rock, based on the given information about the bulk moduli 
of the dry rock, the isotropic mineral constituent and the saturation fluid (Mavko, Mukerji, & Dvorkin, 
2010), while the shear modulus should be the same for wet and dry rock: 
 
Kwet
Ks −Kwet
=
Kdry
Ks −Kdry
+
Kf
∅(Ks −Kf) 
(1.8) 
and  
 
Gwet = Gdry 
(1.9) 
where Kwet, Kdry, Ks and Kf are the effective bulk moduli of the saturated and dry rock in addition to 
the solid mineral and saturation pore fluid, respectively. Gwet and Gdry are the effective shear modulus 
of the saturated and dry rock, which are equal, as the fluid (either liquid or gas) does not have shear 
strength. 
Gassmann fluid substitution works the best giving effective bulk modulus predictions, provided that 
some assumptions are fulfilled: 
- 
the substitution assumes a homogeneous solid mineral modulus (unique Ks) 
- 
pore volume statistical isotropy (Mavko, Mukerji, & Dvorkin, 2010), where in the case of an 
undrained compression of the rock, the substitution assumes a local pore fluid pressure 
equilibrium between the well-connected pore networks (Fabricius, Bächle, & Eberli, 2010). 
- 
most importantly, the substitution is valid in low frequency ranges for the propagating elastic 
wave, where induced temporary pore pressure is equilibrated throughout the pores.  
This explains why Gassmann predictions work perfectly for very low-frequency in-situ seismic data 
(<100 Hz), while the efficiency of predictions deteriorates when frequencies increase towards sonic 
logging ranges (~10 KHz) or ultra-sonic lab measurements (~1 MHz) (Mavko, Mukerji, & Dvorkin, 
2010). 
 
 
4 Therefore, Copenhagen tape water was selected as will be mentioned later in Petrophysical characterization 
workflow. 
==PAGE:13==
 
 
5 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
However, Gassmann’s equation and predictions maybe generalized to the anisotropic case and for the 
mineral heterogeneous case as well (Fabricius, Bächle, & Eberli, 2010). Also, based on the discussion 
of partial and multiphase saturation of the pore fluid and its sensitivity on the seismic waves, the dry 
effective bulk modulus should not be the extremely dry bulk modulus of the rock sample (Mavko, 
Mukerji, & Dvorkin, 2010, pp. 320-321). It has been shown that the first few percent of fluid saturation 
(first few water monolayers) added to an extremely dry rock lowers the effective dry frame modulus 
(weakening).  And investigations also showed fairly accurate Gassmann’s predictions if the value of 
dry effective rock modulus of the frame is used from a slightly moisted (slight liquid saturation) rock 
sample (Kdry). For this reason, and to avoid the artifacts of ultra-dry rock, the dry measurements of this 
study were done at room conditions with a typical relative humidity percent5 (RH).  
1.2.3 
Isoframe Model for Effective-Medium Modelling  
When it comes to predict the effective elastic moduli of a mixture of grains and pores theoretically, we 
generally need to specify: 
- 
the volume fractions of the solid and pore fluid phases 
- 
the individual elastic modulus of each phase 
- 
the geometry of how phases are arranged relative to each other 
The third point is a bit challenging to specify in natural deposited and cemented rocks, therefore the 
best approach is to predict the upper and lower bounds for the elastic moduli that conveys the strongest 
and weakest (most and least stiff) geometric arrangements of the phases. For, simplicity, we will stick 
to an assumption with only two phases; solid stiff phase of the solid grain and the pore saturation fluid 
soft phase which is water. For any other geometric arrangements that fall between those max. (KHS+, 
GHS+, MHS+) and min. (KHS−, GHS−, MHS−) bounds, the result of the effective moduli is supposed to 
fall in between those bounds. 
The best bounds for an isotropic elastic composite porous rock, that give a narrow range of effective 
elastic moduli are the Hashin–Shtrikman bounds (Mavko, Mukerji, & Dvorkin, 2010, p. 169) (Fabricius 
I. L., 2003, p. 1760). The bounds are given by: 
 
KHS± = K1 +
f2
(K2 −K1)−1 + f1 (K1 + 4
3 G1)
−1 
(1.10) 
 
 
GHS± = G1 +
f2
(G2 −G1)−1 + 2f1(K1 + 2G1) (5G1 (K1 + 4
3 G1))
−1 
(1.11) 
 
 
𝑀HS± = KHS± + 4
3 GHS± 
(1.12) 
where K1 and K2 are the bulk moduli of individual phases; G1 and G2 are the shear moduli of individual 
phases; f1and f2 are the volume fractions of each phase.  
With equations (1.10), (1.11) the upper and lower bounds are obtained by interchanging which material 
is termed 1 and which is termed 2. The upper maximum stiff bound is given when the stiff phase (solid 
rock) is termed 1 and accordingly, f1 is the volume fraction of the grain volume (1 −∅). While the 
 
 
5 The relative humidity percentage (RH) is the ratio of the amount of water in the air at a given room temperature 
to the maximum amount it could hold at the same temperature. The room temperature of the ultrasonic 
measurements was around 23℃, and the RH sensor reading was around 38%. 
==PAGE:14==
 
 
6 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
least minimum stiff bound is given by giving the soft phase (pore saturation fluid) the term 1, and f1 is 
the pore volume fraction (∅).  
As shown in Figure 1.2 Hashin–Shtrikman bounds can be interpreted by an assembly of spheres 
surrounded by shells. When, the stiff phase (solid gains) is the outer shell and the soft phase (fluid) is 
the inner sphere, then the composite assembly will be stiff and closer to the upper bound depending on 
volume fractions. Contrarily, when the stiff solid is the inner sphere and the fluid is the outer shell, the 
assembly will tend to be closer to the soft bound. In other words, the solid grains are in suspension in 
the fluid phase. 
 
Figure 1.2: Two-phase composite and its Hashin–Shtrikman bounds physical visualization (Mavko, Mukerji, & Dvorkin, 2010, 
p. 171) 
For a given solid grains and saturation fluid assembly with certain elastic moduli and porosity, the 
stiffness of the assembly is a combination of solid frame and pore fluid stiffnesses, where both are 
bearing the external compression load. However, there are some grains without good grain-to-grain 
contact, therefore the other grains with a grain-to-fluid contact or suspension are also bearing the load. 
(Mavko, Mukerji, & Dvorkin, 2010, p. 226). With compaction, the porosity of the rock will decrease, 
and stiffness increases because more grains become in contact together. This introduces the definition 
of critical porosity (∅𝑐), which is the maximum porosity the rock composite could ever have. So, for ∅ 
< ∅𝑐 the solid grains mainly bear the compaction load, and when ∅ > ∅𝑐, more grains become in 
suspension the fluid phase get stiffed. Here is the hub of the modified Hashin–Shtrikman bounds relative 
to the critical maximum porosity.  
This also introduces the Isoframe effective model assuming that only a fraction of the solid is allocated 
to give a certain frame stiffness (according to the upper HS bound where there is a grain-to-grain 
contact, and such fraction is allocated to the solid frame). While the remaining solid fraction with a 
grain-to-fluid contact is suspended in the saturation pore fluid phase, according to the lower HS bound. 
Such solid fraction is defined as IF fraction. (Fabricius, Høier, Japsen, & Korsbech, 2007) (Fabricius, 
Bächle, & Eberli, 2010). For modelling simplicity, the critical porosity is assumed to be 100% and solid 
grains are homogeneous of single mineralogy (calcite), and the sensitivity of porosity and IF fraction 
on stiffness is shown in Figure 1.3 and Figure 1.4 for water saturated (wet) and air saturated (dry) 
composite porous rock.

9 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
1.3 
Study objective 
 
The objective of the study is to perform petrophysical characterization of 4 limestone samples of 
different pore sizes. With results from the petrophysical characterization and choice of saturation fluid, 
the frequency regime according to Biot’s theory will be assessed for the samples. Simultaneously 
measurements of ultrasonic velocity of the samples (dry and saturated) under variant triaxial stresses 
will be done to confirm the predicted frequency regime by assessing the applicability of Gassmann’s 
fluid substitution theory.
==PAGE:18==
 
10 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
2 
Core Samples Description and Geological Setting 
 
The study was initiated with 6 Upper Cretaceous-Danian limestone core plugs, extracted from Zealand 
region and the existing chalk group for a previous subsurface energy storage project at DTU (Paci, 
Rocchi, & Fabricius, 2017). Due to overloading over 2 samples during the initial dry unconfined 
compression testing trials, 2 samples were not in-tact afterwards. Therefore, only 46 core samples were 
tested and characterized for the entire study work (Figure 2.1).  All samples are vertical in coring 
orientation, coming from two different formations in the chalk group: Copenhagen Limestone 
(København Kalk) and Stevns (Faxe Lime Quarry) Formations (Figure 2.2). However, samples have 
different degree of induration as shown in Table 2.1 that reflects on the diagenetic history of the rock 
and influences the compressive strength thereof (Paci, Rocchi, & Fabricius, 2017) (Hansen, Galsgaard, 
& Foged, 2015). Particularly, sample no. 4 has the least induration so that this sample was a bit 
problematic in handling in different testing steps as will be discussed later about Figure 3.12. 
 
Figure 2.1: The 4 limestone core plugs used for the study. a, b, c, and d are for samples 2, 3, 4 and 6 respectively.

3 
Methodology 
 
This chapter incorporate experimental physics background, steps and measurements that have been 
carried out on the core samples leading to the results and analyses. Also, it includes the mathematical 
calculations and utilized models and equations that have been in use. 
3.1 
Petrophysical characterization workflow 
 
The 4 core samples went through different characterization and lab measurements steps (both in dry 
and tape water-saturated conditions) to quantify the pore structure and distribution of each sample. Tape 
water was selected to be the saturation pore fluid as water in Copenhagen area comes from underground 
aquifers where water is in good equilibrium with carbonate cations. Accordingly, when the core samples 
get in contact with such carbonate-rich water, the chemical interactions with the samples’ carbonate 
content are minimized.  
 
3.1.1 
Gas porosity and Klingenberg corrected permeability (Poro-perm) 
Porosity and Pore/grain Volume 
Porosity (∅) is defined as the ratio of the pore volume to the bulk volume of the porous sample. While 
the bulk volume of the sample (VB) is the total sum of the pore volume (VP) added to the solid grain 
volume (VG). The experimental setup shown in Figure 3.1 is based on the Boyle-Mariotte’s Law; for a 
certain mass of trapped gas, the law states that: 
 
PrefVref
Tref
=
PexpVexp
Texp
 
(3.1) 
where P, V and T stands for the pressure, volume, and temperature of the gas, initially at reference point 
and then later after allowing the gas to expand in extra volume. 
The measurements are done at constant temperature, therefore the temperature in dominators could be 
eliminated. For instance, when the grain volume is the subject to determine in the matrix cup shown in 
==PAGE:20==
 
 
12 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
Figure 3.1.b, the rock sample is placed inside the cup and secured. Then, the source valve is opened to 
pressurize the tank at a reference pressure value Pref. Vref is the tank volume plus piping as well. The 
matrix valve is opened, and the gas is allowed to expand in a new volume which is the sum of the Vref 
plus the volume of the matrix cup VMatrix that contains the subject sample to analyze., reduced by the 
solid grain volume VG.  
  
Figure 3.1: The gas porosimeter (Poro-Perm) used to determine the gas porosity, grain volume and permeability for the 1.5” 
core plugs. a) shows the core holder the top inlet bottom outlet for the nitrogen gas cycle in the device. b) the 1.5” matrix cup 
and calibrated plugs that are used to only measure the grain volume. 
Therefore, equation (3.1) can be rewritten as: 
 
PrefVref = Pexp(Vref + VMatrix −VG) = Pexp(VDead + VP) 
(3.2) 
where the VDead is the entire gas volume outside the pores of the core sample. 
Then,  
 
VG = (1 −Pref
Pexp
) Vref + VMatrix 
(3.3) 
 
Pressure values are directly measured by transducers, and the volumes are determined using self-
calibration using well known volumes of metallic billets (shown in the red rectangle in Figure 3.1.b). 
The difference between the volumes of the billets (the geometrical volume and the precited on from 
a)
b)
==PAGE:21==
 
 
13 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
equation (3.3)) will give an estimate of the error (standard deviation, σ8 [cc]) in the measured grain 
volumes (or pore volumes but for drilled billets placed in the core holder not the matrix cup) from the 
device. Pore and grain volume calculations are two-way road, if one is measured the other can be 
calculated from the bulk volume of the sample. 
The pore volume and porosity were measured in the core holder, just before the permeability 
measurement. While the grain volume was measured again individually at the matric cup. 
Permeability 
The permeability (k in [Darcy or mD]) as a porous rock property determine how much ease the fluids 
can flow through the pore network. Therefore, the calculation of the permeability is driven from Darcy’s 
Law for liquids. Darcy’s flow only applies when the flow is laminar and under steady-state regime. 
 
k = ηQL
A∆P 
(3.4) 
where η is the saturating fluid (liquid) viscosity [c. poise =10-3 Pa.s], Q is the flow rate [ml/s], L is the 
length of the sample or the core plug placed inside the core holder [cm], A is the normal cross-sectional 
area of the cores [cm2], and ∆P is the pressure drop or differential across the sample between up- and 
downstream arrows shown in Figure 3.1 [Atm]. The permeability unit has the dimensions of the area 
[cm2], which is equal to (1 Darcy [D] = 9.869×10-13 m2). 
And because the fluid that is used in the lab is gaseous Nitrogen, which is compressible, Gas Darcy’s 
equation will change into: 
 
kGas = ηQbPbL
A∆PPm
 
(3.5) 
where Qb is the atmospheric gas flow rate [ml/s], Pb is the base or the atmospheric pressure and Pm is 
the mean gas pressure [Atm]. 
The mean pressure is defined as: 
 
Pm = ∆P
2 =
Pup −Pdown
2
 
(3.6) 
 
Due to the compressible nature of the gases, the permeability of the gas in the same porous rock will be 
different at different pressure differentials. The gas permeability decreases when the pressure 
differential is increased. Compared with a liquid at low pressure differences, the gas permeability will 
be higher than that of the liquid, in the same rock. This is because the gas slippage effect at the solid 
pore interface, where the gas molecules still move downstream. Therefore, Klingenberg determined the 
liquid permeability of a certain rock tested with gas flow, provided that infinite high-pressure 
differentials are applied the gas will act as a liquid (minimum compressible volume), and the measured 
permeability will be the liquid one. Klingenberg effect is described through this equation: 
 
kGas = k (1 + b
Pm
) 
(3.7) 
where b is a Klingenberg correction factor and k is the liquid permeability. 
So, for different measured gas permeability points corresponding to different mean pressure values (that 
maintain Darcy laminar and steady state flow), the intercept from a plot of kGas versus the inverse of 
 
 
8 The uncertainty determined from the device calibration was 0.037 [cc], that has been used in error propagation 
in porosity, as described in Appendix D - Error Propagation, Table D.11.

1
Pm, will be equal to the liquid permeability k. This means practically if we can reach 
a mean pressure value approaching infinity (mean pressure inverse is zero that corresponds to the Y-
axis intercept), then the gas is condensed into a liquid and the measured permeability is for the liquid.

Figure 3.2: The Darcy Pressure Stabilization criteria selection settings in the Poro-Perm software. The lower the criterion is,

3.1.2 
NMR Characterization: Total Porosity and Pore Size Distribution 
The nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) characterization is governed by the physical quantum property 
of atoms and molecules that is called Magnetic Spin (spinning around an axis) as shown in Figure 3.4. 
NMR lab devices measure the magnetic response of the protons within the 
hydrogen atoms, as hydrogen is the dominant atom found in pore fluids.  
With an external static magnetic field (z plane) stimulating a random 
spinning proton, the proton starts to react and aligns parallel to the external 
static magnetic field (B0) orientation. Then, protons’ parallel alignment 
rate increases exponentially in certain amount of wait time according to 
equation (3.8), this time is defined as longitudinal relaxation time (T1) or 
spin-lattice relaxation. It is the time when the magnetization reaches 63% 
of its final value and 3 times of this time is the point at which 95% of the 
protons are polarized and aligned to the static magnetic field.  
y = 7.2774x + 39.914

Mz(t) = M0 (1 −e
−t
T1) 
(3.8) 
where Mz(t)  and M0  are the magnitude of magnetization at any time step t  and maximum 
magnetization that could possibly achieved under the external static magnetic field. 
Once protons get aligned in z plane, another oscillating magnetic field (B1) is applied, therefore protons 
change spinning axis orientation to 90 ̊ in x-y plane. When, the external magnetic field is turned off, the 
protons precess but they are no longer in-phase, and the net magnetization decreases in an exponential 
decay. The decay time in the x-y plane is called transverse (spin-spin) relaxation (T2). 
 
Mx−y(t) = M0𝑥−𝑦e
−t
T2 
(3.9) 
where Mx−y(t) is the remaining magnetization magnitude at time step t, while the M0𝑥−𝑦 is the 
magnitude of the transverse magnetization when t = 0 (the time when the 90 ̊ stopped). 
 
Figure 3.5: Spinning and Percession of a hydrogen proton under static magnetic field (B0) in z plane followed by an oscillating 
one (B1) in x-y plane. (McPhee, Reed, & Zubizarreta, 2015) 
In NMR devices, there are receiver coils (antennas) to measure magnetization signal in both z 
(longitudinal relaxation) and x-y (transverse) directions. Such decays and two relaxation times 
implicitly describe the pore-size distribution containing the protons of the pore fluid. In oil and gas 
applications, to get a full spectrum for the T1 this implies a longer acquisition time or maybe stationary 
mode in open hole logging operations, which is not that efficient (Lake & Holstein, n.d.) (McPhee, 
Reed, & Zubizarreta, 2015, p. 659). Therefore, the T2 measurements are more time efficient, and this is 
the approach adopted in this study.  
The technique for obtaining the Transverse T2 spectrum is called Spin-Echo. The protons are tipped into 
the x-y (transverse plane) by the initial 90 ̊ magnetic pulse. Then, subsequent 180 ̊ pulses are applied to 
flip the spinning polarity but still in the x-y plane and at a fixed time interval, defined as echo spacing 
TE. Those 180 ̊ pulses, rephase the spinning axis polarity protons that are getting out-of-phase after the 
initial 90 ̊ pulse. Accordingly, rephased protons will produce a detectable signal by the receiver coil, 
which is called a Spin Echo. In practice, a series of pulses (CMPG sequence) is used to create an echo 
train, in which the echo amplitude decays exponentially according to a time constant T2, which is 
governed by equation (3.9) and described in Figure 3.6. Such CMPG sequence technique is useful to 
counteract dephasing due to magnetic field inhomogeneities that cause spin interactions. 
==PAGE:26==
 
 
18 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
 
Figure 3.6: CMPG pulse sequence (top) and alternating polarization (T1 relaxation) (bottom) followed by echo train (T2 
relaxation). (Lake & Holstein, n.d.) 
One of the benefits of NMR-measured porosity is that it is independent of the mineralogy of the 
formation rock. The echo amplitudes depend only on the hydrogen content of the formation fluids and 
are not affected by the rock bulk properties (Allen, Flaum, Ramakrishnan, & Fairhurst). 
Transverse (T2) NMR relaxation is sensitive to localized motions of hydrogen nuclei and their 
interactions with the surroundings that make their spins dephased. There are three relaxation 
mechanisms: 
- 
Bulk fluid relaxation based on the viscosity of the fluid bulk, T2B 
- 
Relaxation due to molecular diffusion and mutual collisions in heterogenous magnetic field 
gradient, T2D 
- 
Surface relaxation due to interactions with pore solid walls, T2S 
With assumption that even after mutual collisions between protons, the diffused protons will eventually 
collide with the pore wall solid surface. The relaxation is rapid when the collision probability is high, 
therefore in smaller pores the relaxation is faster, compared with larger pores as illustrated in Figure 
3.7.  
 
Figure 3.7: 3D packing of consolidated microporous grains, with large macropores and small micropores. (Allen, Flaum, 
Ramakrishnan, & Fairhurst) 
==PAGE:27==
 
 
19 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
The eventual total NMR response measured by the device, is a contribution (superposition) of all three 
mechanisms.  
 
1
T2
= 1
T2B
+ 1
T2D
+ 1
T2S
 
(3.10) 
 
However, assuming the applied magnetic field in the lab setup (quite like Figure 3.8) is homogeneous, 
the relaxation time is significantly governed by the surface relaxation. 
 
1
T2
= 1
T2S
= ρT2
S
VP
 
(3.11) 
where T2 [ms] is the lab measured relaxation time, ρT2 [µm/s] is defined as the surface relaxivity which 
is a characteristic property for each mineralogy and its ability to relax (dephase) hydrogen protons upon 
mutual collisions. 
S
VP [µm−1] which is the specific pore surface area (the ratio between the pore total 
surface area over the pore volume VP ).  
The specific pore surface area, as will be quantitively defined later with equation (4.3), depends on the 
geometrical shape of the pore. For instance, if the pores are spherical, the specific area will be given as: 
 
SP = S
VP
= 3
r 
(3.12) 
where 3 is a number resulted from the area over the volume for a sphere. For a cylindrical pore, the 
number will be 2. 
This number is defined as a pore shape factor FS, and equation (3.12) could be rewritten as: 
 
SP = FS
r  
(3.13) 
 
Then, equation (3.11), will give the value for transverse relaxation time as: 
 
T2 =
r
ρT2FS
 
(3.14) 
 
 
Figure 3.8: NMR lab setup; to the left is a cross-section through the core holder and the magnetic field chamber, while on the 
right is the software interface and external look of the NMR chamber. (McPhee, Reed, & Zubizarreta, 2015) 
==PAGE:28==

Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
As described in (Xu, et al., 2019), the NMR transverse relaxation time could be non-linearly correlated 
to the pore radius trapping a certain fluid saturation percent. If all pores are fully saturated with for 
instance water, then the output NMR relaxation distribution (which is called Pore Size Distribution) 
will convey the total pore volume in the tested core plug. In other words, NMR will result in the total 
porosity value. For heterogenous rock sample, which is very typical to find, the total pore volume is a 
combination from wide pore size ranges. Accordingly, the response of the NMR will respond to that 
and will result in a specific distribution of the T2 time which corresponds to a fraction of the pore 
volume inside the core plug (fractional porosity) that gives this relaxation time range. Therefore, the 
shortest relaxation time range says how much volume is represented by small pores, and vice versa. The 
NMR results for the 4 limestone samples were as following in shown in Figure 3.9. The NMR 
measurement was done on saturated samples (wet), after samples have been saturated with tape water 
under vacuum for 14 days, and then samples taken out of vacuum to be weighted and handled with wet 
tissue to avoid any in situ saturation alterations. Before wet weight determination, the samples were 
fully covered with a well-known weight of parafilm (that acts as a tight moisture-barrier). Then, the 
covered samples were placed in the NMR chamber and tested, and later the total signal response for 
covered samples were reduced by subtracting the background signal that comes for the parafilm itself. 
 
Figure 3.9: NMR measured pore size distribution and the cumulative total porosity for water saturated samples. The wet 
(saturated samples) were covered in parafilm after vacuum saturation for 14 days, that holds water inside the sample and 
prevent any desaturation. Therefore, after samples measurements, the same weight of the parafilm was measured and got 
subtracted as a background signal from the main signal. The results represent the net signal for core plugs only.

where C′ [µm/s] is a conversion parameter the implicitly includes the surface relaxivity and shape

integrating the MICP (mercury intrusion) pore size distribution data. Therefore, MICP will be discussed 
next. 
3.1.3 
MICP Pore Size Distribution 
The mercury intrusion capillary pressure (MICP) is carried out using mercury porosimeter analyzer, 
where mercury is gradually intruded under pressure into the pore network of a little fraction representing 
the rock sample. The mercury as a non-wetting phase (very high surface tension γ (480 erg/cm2) and 
contact angle θ (140 ̊) will fill the pore, and with pressure and gradual intruded volume monitoring this 
will reflect on the radius of the pores. Firstly, the capillary pressure is defined as the pressure difference 
between two immiscible phases (in oil and gas applications the non-wetting phase pressure minus the 
wetting phase pressure). The capillary difference is a result of the variation of the fluids resistance to 
spread and create new incremental surface contact. The work required to produce 1 cm2 of a surface is 
defined as the surface tension (γ). Assuming a cylindrical pore shape, the mercury is invading a pore 
radius (r) and with length of (l), the are occupied by the intruded volume is: 
 
A = 2πrl 
(3.17) 
 
The work required to create this additional contact area could be written in terms of the surface tension: 
 
W = 2πrl γ cos θ 
(3.18) 
 
Introducing cosθ, is to indicate the parallel force component the push mercury forward to come in 
contract with the grain surface. Another equivalent work exerted on the mercury volume is given as: 
 
W = Pπr2l 
(3.19) 
where P is the mercury non-wetting pressure (it is not a pressure difference as the mercury is invading 
dry pore space). 
From equations (3.18) and (3.19), the capillary pressure is defined as: 
 
P = 2 γ cosθ
r
 
(3.20) 
 
And if dV is the volume element of all pores with radii between r and r + dr, then 
 
dV = Dv(r)dr 
(3.21) 
where DV is the pore volume distribution function (pore volume per unit radius interval). 
With differentiation of equation (3.20), it turns into: 
 
Pdr + rdP = 0 
(3.22) 
 
From equations (3.21) and (3.22) 
 
dV = −Dv(r) r
P dP 
(3.23) 
 
And then, 
 
Dv(r) = P
r
dV
dP 
(3.24) 
==PAGE:30==
 
 
22 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
where the negative sign has been eliminated because the volume/pressure derivative is negative 
(elemental mercury volume filling pores decreases with increased pressure.) 
Equation (3.24) gives the pore volume distribution function (frequency) for each pore range, and with 
recording the pressure and volume variations, the corresponding pore radius can be calculated, and the 
pore size distribution will be obtained.  
3.1.4 
BET Specific Pore Surface Area by Low Temperature Nitrogen Adsorption (LTNA) 
This experimental technique is utilized to characterize the quantitively analyze the surface and pore size 
in porous materials, based on Gas Adsorption physics and chemistry. Adsorption is the enrichment of 
one or more components in an interfacial layer, which is the gas/solid interface (LOWELL, SHIELDS, 
THOMAS, & THOMMES, 2004). The solid is called adsorbent and the gas is called adsorbate. The 
amount of adsorbed gas will depend on the temperature, pressure and interaction potential between the 
vapor adsorbate and the solid adsorbent surface. Based on the strength of the interactions, the adsorption 
process can be categorized as chemical or physical adsorption. Chemical is the irreversible adsorption 
interaction, while the physical is reversible. In the physical adsorption, there is no structural changes to 
the solid surface during adsorption measurements. Unlike the chemisorption, the fully reversible 
physical adsorption allows multilayer adsorption and takes place rapidly with no activation energy 
requirement, which is an advantage that the pores could be filled with gas vapor, and this will be used 
to calculate the pore size and its distribution (LOWELL, SHIELDS, THOMAS, & THOMMES, 2004). 
Moreover, the adsorption can take place anywhere on the solid surface not at restricted spots like the 
chemisorption. This is an advantage to be able to estimate the total surface area. Therefore, the physical 
adsorption is most suitable for pore size characterization.  
At sufficiently low temperatures (typically around the boiling temperature of the adsorptive) a dense 
monolayer of gas molecules (the gas used is Nitrogen) is formed at pressures P far below the saturation 
pressure P0 (the pressure at which there is an equilibrium between the vapor molecules at the solid 
interface and bulk condensed phase of the vapor). Then, a multilayer adsorbed film with a liquid like 
density builds up on strongly adsorbing substrates as P0 is approached. 
The success of theories to measure surface areas depends upon their ability to predict the number of 
adsorbate molecules required to cover the solid with a single molecular layer. Given that, the cross-
sectional area of each molecule or the effective area covered by each adsorbed molecule on the surface 
is defined. The Brunauer, Emmett and Teller (BET) theory, is assuming that the uppermost molecules 
in the adsorbed stack is in dynamic equilibrium with the vapor. And as the equilibrium is dynamic, the 
actual location of the covered surface is dynamic or variant, but the number of molecules in each layer 
is constant. From those assumption the theory leads to the following BET correlation: 
 
1
W (P0
P −1)
=
1
WmC + C −1
WmC ( P
P0
) 
(3.25) 
where W is the weight of adsorbed gas at a relative pressure 
P
P0. Wm is the weight of adsorbate 
constituting a monolayer covering the surface. The term C (BET C constant) is related to the energy of 
adsorption in the first adsorbed layer and consequently its value is an indication of the magnitude of the 
adsorbent/adsorbate interactions. 
From BET equation, a linear plot between 
1
W(P0
P −1)  and 
P
P0  could be created for a multipoint 
measurement (standard is a minimum of three points) for a typical BET experiment. Then, from the 
intercept and slope, the monolayer weight Wm is obtained: 
 
Slope = C −1
WmC 
(3.26) 
 
==PAGE:31==
 
 
23 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
 
Intercept =
1
WmC  
(3.27) 
 
Both equations (3.26) and (3.27) combined, will result in: 
 
Wm = (Slope + Intercept)−1 
(3.28) 
The second BET step is to calculate the surface area of the tested porous sample. This required a known 
molecular cross section area of the nitrogen adsorbate molecule. The total surface area is given as: 
 
ST = WmNAcs
Mwt
 
(3.29) 
where N  is Avogadro Number (6.02×1023 [molecules/mole]). Mwt  is Nitrogen molecular weight 
(28.013 [gm/mole]). Acs is the molecular cross-sectional area, and this is related to the BET C constant. 
For a Nitrogen hexagonal close packed monolayer at a temperature of 77K, the cross-sectional area is 
16.2 [Å2]. 
Then, from equation (3.29), the specific surface area SBET [m2/gm] is obtained from the total surface 
area ST [m2]and the mass of the porous material that has been tested in [gm]: 
 
SBET = ST
w 
(3.30)

3.1.5 
Vacuum Saturation versus Pressurized Flooding 
After vacuum saturation and before carrying out all associated wet measurements (weighting, NMR, 
sonic measurements under triaxial loading), the calculated water saturation values were not 100%. The 
saturation was calculated based on the weight difference between the wet and dry samples. 
 
Sw =
mwet −mdry
ρwaterVP
=
mwet −mdry
ρwater∅VB
 
(3.31) 
where mwet and mdry are the wet and dry mass of the sample [gm], ρwater the tape water density that 
is taken as 0.998 gm/cc (Fabricius & Andreassen, 2010), VP is the pore volume [cc] which is obtained 
from the product of gas porosity ∅ times the bulk volume VB. 
Therefore, this saturation deficiency was probably due to the very tight pore network, for which vacuum 
was not enough to make water invade in. Also, this was supported by the fact that NMR total porosity 
value was less than the Gas expansion porosity from Poro-Perm as described in Table 3.4.

3.1.6 
NMR and MICP Pore Size Distribution Convergence 
As mentioned from NMR and MICP, the outcome is a pore size distribution (PSD) but with different 
presentation style. NMR is the fractional porosity (Y-axis) versus the transverse relaxation time (X-
axis) that correspond to a certain fraction of pore size, which is capable of relaxing hydrogen nuclei in 
the saturation water within such milli seconds of T2 time. On the other hand, MICP pore throat11 size 
 
 
11 Some literatures use PTSD, which is also a pore size distribution, as the injected mercury is experiencing the 
capillary resistance mainly at the throat of the pore. Therefore, it could be said that the pore size distribution is 
more affected by the pore throat radius, which is likely to be close to the pore size itself. 
a)
b)
==PAGE:35==
 
 
27 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
distribution (PTSD) is given as a pore size distribution function (Y-axis) that represents the repetition 
frequency of a certain pore size range versus the pore radius or diameter in µm (X-axis).  
MICP is done on a little fraction of the same dry core rock, while NMR is done on the entire bulk of 
the saturated sample. So, NMR PSD is more representative for the sample. But the subject 4 samples in 
the study seem to be fairly homogenous, therefore the MICP PTSD could be considered as an equivalent 
to NMR PSD. This point is a bottom line that leads back to equation (3.16), that can help find a direct 
non-linear relation between the pore radius (straightforward from MICP) and the T2 relaxation time 
(from NMR). This is done through convergence and least squared residuals optimization to find the best 
convergence parameters in equation (3.16). The detailed calculations are discussed in Appendix A - 
NMR and MICP PSD Analysis and Convergence Calculations. 
 
Figure 3.13: The PSD convergence between the NMR and MICP according to equation (3.16), when relaxation time [ms] is 
converted to the matching pore radius [µm] according to calculated approximate convergence parameter C′ and n. The results 
are normalized to the maximum value to make the maximum PSD even between samples and give more sense for the 
comparison. 
The final convergence results are shown in Figure 3.13, which is relatively a good match, except for 
some exceptions for sample# 3m where there is a peak for high contribution from pore sizes in the range 
of 5-7 µm. Also, the average dominant pore radius that corresponds to the highest PSD frequency, is 
quite the same among all samples (≈ 1.6 µm), but the difference is obvious in the covered pore radius 
range under each curve. The larger the pore radius range is, the larger the area under the curve will be, 
and the more sample heterogeneity will be seen. An attempt to quantitively assess the heterogeneity, it 
to calculate the area under the PSD curves, and determine how much of the area is above and below the 
average dominant pore radius. According to equation (3.16), the determined convergence parameters 
are included in the following table, in addition to the dominant pore radius and the average dominant 
radius:

However, the quality of the converged PSD should be compared with the original measured NMR PSD, 
by plotting both with the MICP PTSD as shown in Figure 3.14. It is obvious that only sample# 2 the 
convergence was not that good compared with other samples. Therefore, the area under the curve will 
be more representative when it is taken from the measured NMR curve. For the other 3 samples, the 
area of the curve is determined by the converged NMR PSD curve. The reason for choosing NMR curve 
oven the MICP, is as explained earlier the NMR is more representing all bulk volume of the sample, 
not just a couple of grams of the sample like the MICP case. 
The area is approximate, as it was calculated with the trapezoidal method. And the area fraction below 
the average dominant radius relative to the area fraction above it will indicate the homogeneity. The 
more deviation the area fractions have from 50% ration, the less homogeneous the sample is. 
Accordingly, the samples are ranked in a descending homogeneity order as following: 6 > 3 > 2 > 4. 
This is simulated in Figure 3.15.  
Also, the homogeneity is strongly relevant to the covered pore radii range under the PSD curve. From 
this point, it is also clear that NMR PSD (which is better representing the entire bulk sample) samples# 
6 and 3 cover smaller pore radii range (0.1-10 µm), while samples# 2 and 4 exceed that range, whether 
by smaller pores or larger pores. This will be better supported with applying Kozeny’s permeability 
correlation later (equations (4.4) and (4.7)), which works best for the homogeneous porous medium.

be much biased with the odd high pore radius for sample# 4.

likely because the vuggy surface of the sample compared with other samples as shown in Figure 2.1. 
Those large external pores are probably included in the Poro-Perm gas porosity measured value, when 
the sample was placed inside the core holder and those vugs were sealed by the rubber membrane inside 
the holder to act as an extra pore volume fraction. While the same fraction after sample saturation and 
being wrapped in the parafilm, it was not occupied by water as a saturation fluid containing the 
hydrogen protons to relax and contribute to the NMR PSD signal. Therefore, the Gas porosity was 
probably a better estimate for the pore volume over the bulk volume that implicitly includes those vugs.

Figure 3.15: Sample PSD homogeneity is done by assessing Area under the NMR PSD curve determined by trapezoidal 
method. When fractions of pores above and below the average dominant radius (1.6 µm) approach 50%, this indicates more

Secondly, the total NMR cumulative porosity post flooding was less than the vacuum saturated value 
with 3.4 % porosity units, for this particular sample# 4. While the other samples if the post flooding 
porosity is not a slightly larger than the vacuum saturated value e.g., samples# 2 and 3, it will be almost 
the same e.g., sample# 6. The reason for that, is the significant change in the geometrical pore structure 
of the exterior body of sample# 4, as it is shown in Figure 3.12.b.  
So, as an attempt to track the change in the pore size distribution for sample# 4 after flooding as shown 
in Figure 3.17, the area under the curve was calculated according to a couple of cutoffs at certain pore 
radii, to show how much fraction of those radii still contributes to the pore size distribution, and how 
much contribution comes from probably new created pores that could be through microfractures or 
enlarged pores from which the solids grains were lost. Before that, the NMR convergence with MICP 
for flooded sample# 4 revealed new convergence parameters included in Table 3.6 according to 
equation (3.16). For estimating the area under the curves, central or average pore radius (or 
corresponding T2 time) should be selected. Here, the logarithmic mean for NMR PSD was selected to 
represent the central time/pore radius, and areas above/below this value will approximately convey the 
percent or fraction of large/small pores in the sample. 
The central logarithmic mean T2lm time [ms] and pore radius r2lm [µm] are derived from those equations: 
 
T2lm = e
(∑(ln T2i×∅i)
∑∅i
) 
(3.32) 
 
 
rlm = e
(∑(ln ri×∅i)
∑∅i
) 
(3.33) 
where, T2i and ri are the incremental T2 time and its closest matching pore radius from MICP data. ∅i 
is the incremental normalized fractional porosity from NMR.

to any induced micro fractures.  On the other hand, the pore radius range 0.7-1.8 µm, lost 5% of its 
fraction compared to the previous vacuum saturated distribution when the sample was still in full tact.

3.2 
 Triaxial Compression Loading and Acoustic Measurements 
 
The objective of this test is to see the rock mechanical reaction under different ascending load steps, 
while ultrasonic elastic waves are going through. The speed of elastic waves is the indicator for how 
composite rock strength (pore fluid + solid frame) is increasing with increased compression. Owing to 
the relatively low compressional strength of the limestone samples (an average value of 5 MPa for H-2 
and H-3 induration degrees (Hansen, Galsgaard, & Foged, 2015)) that did lead to two samples’ failure 
in the initial preparatory experimental setup, the multistage triaxial testing was applied where the 
confinement radial horizontal pressure (σ2,3) was increased equally to the axial load (σ1). The highest 
mean hydrostatic pressure was 3.6 MPa. 
3.2.1 
Test Equipment and Workflow 
The experimental setup as shown in Figure 3.20, the setup consists of two vertical pistons that contain 
the acoustic transmitter and receiver. The pistons are applying the axial vertical stress, while the 
confining sleeve (called Hoek/biaxial cell (McPhee, Reed, & Zubizarreta, 2015)) surrounding the core 
sample provides the radial horizontal stress. The axial stress was monitored in terms of load force (KN), 
which was converted to stress (MPa) using the sample’s measured cross-section area to align with the 
radial stress pressure in KPa.  
 
Figure 3.20: Triaxial test equipment and Acoustic oscilloscope; a) and b) show the loading pistons with core sample in-
between, which are also housing for the piezoelectric crystals transmitting/receiving the sonic vibrations. The transparent 
hoses/syringe are connected to the pistons where existing internal channels allow water injection and displace trapped from 
the piston/sample contact surface for wet measurements. c) is the confining sleeve operated by hydraulic pump in e) and the 
cross-section profile shows the internal setup of the Hoek cell. f) shows the oscilloscope interface that visualize the elastic 
vibrations into voltage amplitude train versus microseconds. g) an overall setup for all individual equipment connected 
 
a)
b)
c)
                            g)
d)
e)
g)
f)
==PAGE:43==
 
 
35 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
Test Methodology 
1. Initially, the lower piston was placed in, and then fastening a support collar on its perimeter to 
carry the heavy Hoek sleeve till reaching enough radial loads that keep the sleeve hanged 
around the sample. Also, the support collar position was selected with attention to keep the rock 
sample centered along the length of the internal rubber membrane, therefore there is an even 
radial pressure distribution over the core length.  
2. Then, the upper piston was placed inside the sleeve and on top of the core sample. Then, such 
setup composite is carefully centered along the loading arm axis for the axial load unit, then 
levelled to make sure everything is in-level, and the forces direction are perpendicular. 
3. After that, during the first load step, the pistons were set to just tap the sample and same amount 
of axial load was translated into a radial pressure. During this step the collar support was kept 
in place, carrying the sleeve’s weight.  
4. Then, increasing the loads was done gradually, and simultaneously between the axial and radial 
loads. The collar support was removed. Once the target load was reached, the sample was left 
for stress stabilization for 15-30 minutes, which was also indicated by stable readings values. 
This is to make sure that the sample is isostatically stressed (McPhee, Reed, & Zubizarreta, 
2015). 
5. The difference between the wet and dry measurements was the action of syringe and water 
injection to displace the air at the contact surface of the core sample and the piston. This was 
done to make sure that, there is not air to attenuate the acoustic wave energy, giving misleading 
results. 
6. Also, the wet measurements were done on the samples wrapped in parafilm, that was necessary 
for the NMR measurements. So, the parafilm wrap was removed at the circular ends of each 
sample while the long side warp was not. 
7. Then, the oscilloscope and the associated software were used to trigger the elastic waves 
(compressional and shear) and to visualize and record the received vibrations/voltage back into 
3 wave trains: P-wave and 2 shear-waves. 
8. Then, after the last measurement, the unloading was done also gradually and simultaneously, 
till core sample was free to be retrieved out. 
3.2.2 
Saturated (Wet) Primary Results  
Based on the loading sequence listed in Table 3.7 for each wet sample; where the applied load is the 
axial force reading in kN, which is converted to stress σ1 in MPa by dividing over the cross-sectional 
area and the Applied Radial Pressure σ3 is the reading of the biaxial Hoek sleeve pump in kPa. After 
isostatic stabilization time, the elastic waves were triggered and recorded. Each recording includes 3 
wave trains for each load step: compressional (P) wave and Shear 1 and 2 (S1, S2) waves. For instance, 
for wet sample# 3 at the last load step, the full length of recorded wave trains was as shown in Figure 
3.21. 
3.2.3 
Dry Primary Results 
The load sequence on dry samples was done to be as close as possible to the stabilized wet load values, 
as listed in Table 3.8, and similarly to the wet measurements (except for the injection of water by syringe 
along the contact surface between the pistons and sample face) the waves were triggered and recorded 
after load stabilization. For instance, for the dry sample# 3 at the last load step, the full length of 
recorded wave trains was as shown in Figure 3.22.

The first wave arrival picking-up is the point of interest from those measurements. Once the wave travel 
time is defined, the phase velocity is directly calculated from the measured length of each sample and 
with a defined bulk density ρB [gm/cc] the three elastic moduli of the rock at each load step can be 
easily derived using equations (1.3) and (1.4). However, the recorded wave trains like the ones shown 
in previous Figure 3.21 and Figure 3.22 are showing first arrivals that include the travel time of the 
wave through the external circuit of the experimental setup. This extra travel time (lag time) must be 
corrected for and removed from each individual phase travel time 
To determine the lag time, the sonic setup must be calibrated to find the corresponding travel time that 
will be recorded when there is a zero-length sample placed between the pistons (Standard Test Method 
for Laboratory Determination of Pulse Velocities and Ultrasonic Elastic Constants of Rock). This has 
been implemented by using three metallic sample sets of different lengths as described in Appendix B 
- Travel Time Calibration (Circuit Lag Time). The lag time values were obtained as following: 
 
Table 3.9: Determined lag time values after calibration the experimental setup using three metallic sets. 
Lag Time (Δtp)  
[μs] 
Lag Time (Δts1) 
 [μs] 
Lag Time (Δts2)  
[μs] 
2.85 
7.27 
6.58 
 
Moreover, as shown in previous Figure 3.21 and Figure 3.22, the picked-up value of the P-wave first 
arrival was very straight forward. P-wave first arrival is at the first deviation from the zero-voltage line, 
and because it is the fastest wave phase, its piezoelectric crystal can pick up a well-defined first arrival. 
Therefore, the uncertainty in the calculated compression modulus M [GPa] is coming only from the 
bulk density value and the length measurements of the sample, not the picked up first arrival. However, 
for shear waves it is very challenging because the received signal is obscured by other vibrations due 
to transducers ringing or interference with reflected and/or converted P-to-S waves. Therefore, the shear 
wave arrivals have uncertainty13 in picking-up, even after adjusting the oscilloscope’s sensitivity to 
minimize the interference with P-wave or with any associated noises.  
The utilized oscilloscope (shown in Figure 3.20.f) is manufactured by KEYSIGHT Technologies with a 
bandwidth of 100 [MHz]. The bandwidth is a key factor in acoustic measurements as it is functioning 
as a filter for the frequencies the oscilloscope can accurately pick. Therefore, the oscilloscope is like a 
low-pass filter that can pass and receive frequencies from 0 Hz up to the bandwidth limit value. Thus, 
selecting too much higher bandwidth will not provide the representative wave and too low bandwidth 
will pick a lot of noises.  
3.2.4 
Isoframe Effective Model to Minimize First Shear Arrival Picking-up Uncertainty 
Isoframe model as discussed earlier (equations (1.12) - (1.16)) is helpful to introduce a guidance for the 
right range of time in which the true first shear arrival lies.  
1. From Isoframe effective model equations, for an assumption of any typical IF value for the rock 
frame, it is possible to get an initial estimate of the compressional modulus (P-wave modulus, 
M [GPa]) by calculating bulk KHS± [GPa] and shear modulus GHS± [GPa] from equations 
(1.13), (1.14) and (1.15), (1.16) respectively. This will be termed (IF Modulus Value). Those 
equations need input information about the porosity of the sample and the constituents’ elastic 
 
 
13 See Appendix C - Uncertainty in Shear First Arrival Picking-up 
==PAGE:47==
 
 
39 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
properties. With the assumption that the samples have only two possible constituents; 100% 
calcite mineralogy (average carbonate content is listed in Table 2.1 and the pore fluid (water 
for wet and air for dry measurements) is filling the entire pore volume. The input independent 
variables were used as listed in Table 3.10 and measured gas porosity was used as listed in 
Table 3.1. 
2. Then, from the wet/dry mass and calculated dimensions the wet/dry bulk density is obtained. 
From the sample length and the picked-up first arrival for the P-wave, the P-wave phase 
velocity is calculated, and through equation (1.3) a corresponding compressional modulus value 
is determined (Measured Modulus). 
3. After that, by equating the two compressional modulus values (Solver Excel add-in), the actual 
IF value, that allows the sample’s frame to bear the applied load at each step, will be obtained. 
IF value is a general independent variable or property of the rock frame, therefore it is 
generalized for any sort of load (shear, uniaxial or triaxial) 
4. Then, after finding the actual IF value, the Isoframe modelled shear modulus will be modified 
accordingly. And with the defined bulk density and sample’s length the shear wave first arrival 
should be picked up at a reasonable arrival time that results in a relatively matching measured 
shear modulus value through equation (1.4). 
Table 3.10:The bulk and shear elastic moduli of the three possible constituents of the wet/dry sample. Those values were used 
as input in all calculations. (FJÆR, M. HOLT, HORSRUD, & RAAEN, 2008)

Those steps were implemented for the two wet measurements (vacuum saturated and post flooding) as 
well as the dry measurements. The following table’s header summarizes the calculations sequence. 
From dimensions, bulk volume is obtained and accordingly the wet/dry bulk density. From the given 
constituent elastic modulus and gas porosity values, the calculated Isoframe model section is evaluated 
depending on an initial typical IF value as an assumption. Then, P-wave velocity from length and net 
P-first arrival time (lag time is subtracted). Excel solver will find the matching IF value to make the 
moduli difference cell zero. Then, the shear wave trains will be investigated to find the reasonable shear 
first arrivals that matches the measured shear moduli with the Isoframe modelled ones.

As a double check up on the shear first arrival pick-up quality, the Poisson’s ratio was obtained for each 
sample at each load step when it was clear to pick up a first arrival deflection. The Poisson’s ratio was 
calculated according to the following equation with the calculated sonic phase wave velocities:  
 
υ =

(3.34) 
where 𝑉𝑝 and 𝑉𝑠 are the sonic velocity for the p and shear waves. 
And to implement the calculated elastic moduli among Hashin-Shtrikman bounds as described in Figure 
1.3, an estimated porosity value is required at each load step. With triaxial loading, the pore volume 
and grains are compressed against each other, which in turn result in porosity reduction. The reduction 
in porosity can be defined from the volumetric strain under the hydrostatic stress. Such hydrostatic 
stress that corresponds to the bulk elastic modulus is obtained as a mean effective stress P′ [MPa].

The maximum mean stress values were not that high (maximum around 3.6 [MPa]), and most likely all 
samples were in the elastic region without any pore collapse initiation. This resulted in a very slight 
change in the porosity if data is plotted on Hashin-Shtrikman bounds like Figure 1.3 and Figure 1.4 
(standard deviation for the calculated reduced porosity values from the 5 stress steps was 1e-4 and 9e-
5 porosity units for dry and wet samples).

4 
Results and Discussion 
 
For each sample, whether dry or wet, there were 5 increasing load steps (a-e), where the ultrasonic 
waves were emitted and recorded. For dry samples at the lowest load step where the positions were just 
slightly tapping the core plug, there were so much attenuated or noisy wave trains, that completely 
obscured the first arrivals of the different phases. This is likely due to any uneven spots on the sample 
cross-sectional face, that trapped air which in turn attenuated the wave peaks. Therefore, there are only 
4 sets of recorded waves for dry samples# 3, 4 and 6. The results were as plotted in [Figure 4.1 - Figure 
4.12]. The picked first arrivals and corresponding calculated phase wave velocities in addition to 
Poisson’s Ration are summarized in Figure 4.13, Figure 4.14 and Figure 4.15 versus the mean 
hydrostatic stress. 
From such summary, the following observations have been highlighted: 
- 
The P-wave increasing slope does make sense when higher stresses are applied. The more the 
compaction of the core sample, the closer the solid grains become, the less the attenuation will 
be (indicated by the gradual increase of voltage amplitude in the recorded wave trains), and 
ultimately the faster the wave will be received. This is valid for either wet (water saturated) or 
dry conditions. 
- 
On the other hand, the S-Wave should be more or less the same, as long as there is no significant 
change in the solid frame volume. And as long as the S-Wave does not propagate in the 
saturation fluid, the S1 and S2 wave velocities should be in the same limited range. This is 
considerably valid in recorded S2-Wave and the associated first wave arrivals, rather than the 
S1-Wave. 
- 
Regarding the Poisson’s Ratio derived from equation (3.34), it should be a constant value for 
the same core sample with certain saturation conditions. However, due to the uncertainty in the 
calculated wave velocities, or the inevitable irregularities in samples’ dimensions or any little 
misalignment of the pistons, the Poisson’s Ratio is not that fixed. For a typical limestone, the 
Poisson’s Ratio could be in the range of (0-0.3) (FJÆR, M. HOLT, HORSRUD, & RAAEN, 
2008, p. 437). Therefore, the quality of the picked-up first arrivals would be better if the points 
in d and e graphs in Figure 4.13, Figure 4.14 and Figure 4.15 are less scattered around 0.3. 
Accordingly, S2-wave arrivals better fulfill this condition as well. 
From, those observations, S2 first arrivals are better representatives for the shear wave. Also, in most 
recorded shear wave trains, the first arrival peak of the S2 was steep and obvious compared with the 
S1. Therefore, S2-Wave is adopted to be the propagated shear wave, all dependent calculations i.e., 
shear modulus, will adopt S2-Wave. 
==PAGE:50==
 
 
42 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
4.1 
Vacuum Saturated (Wet) Samples 
 
Figure 4.1: Vacuum Saturated (wet) Sample# 2 recorded wave trains at ascending load steps. Time scale shows the significant 
first arrivals and peaks (0-100 [µS]) versus the measured voltage amplitude [v]. The bold data labels are the values that has 
been used to calculate the sonic velocities after subtracting corresponding lag times listed in Table 3.9. The first P-arrival is 
definitely at the first deflection from zero voltage line after the transmitted amplitude. The first shear arrival was selected at 
zero voltage line for an upward-crossing voltage peak, based on the margins of Isoframe modelled shear modulus values Ghs, 
so the measured shear value (Gs1 and Gs2) from equation (1.4) matches. Other adjacent shear first arrivals are also sort of 
probable first arrivals that introduce the uncertainty margins for the selected time value as discussed in Appendix C - 
Uncertainty in Shear First Arrival Picking-up.

Figure 4.6: Dry (post oven at 60℃) Sample# 3 recorded wave trains at ascending load steps after Vacuum Sat. wet 
measurements. Time scale shows the significant first arrivals and peaks (0-100 [µS]) versus the measured voltage amplitude

Figure 4.7: Dry (post oven at 60℃) Sample# 4 recorded wave trains at ascending load steps after Vacuum Sat. wet 
measurements. Time scale shows the significant first arrivals and peaks (0-100 [µS]) versus the measured voltage amplitude 
[v]. The recorded wave at first least load step was highly obscured with probably trapped piston/core interface air. The bold 
data labels are the values that has been used to calculate the sonic velocities after subtracting corresponding lag times listed in 
Table 3.9. The first P-arrival is definitely at the first deflection from zero voltage line after the transmitted amplitude. The first 
shear arrival was selected at zero voltage line for an upward-crossing voltage peak, based on the margins of Isoframe modelled 
shear modulus values Ghs, so the measured shear value (Gs1 and Gs2) from equation (1.4) matches. Other adjacent shear first 
arrivals are also sort of probable first arrivals that introduce the uncertainty margins for the selected time value as discussed in 
Appendix C - Uncertainty in Shear First Arrival Picking-up.

Figure 4.11: Post Flooding (PF Wet) Sample# 4 recorded wave trains at ascending load steps. Time scale shows the significant 
first arrivals and peaks (0-100 [µS]) versus the measured voltage amplitude [v]. The first least load step (a) had significantly 
obscured and attenuated voltage response in P and S waves, respectively. The reason for that is the considerable vug in one of 
the sample’s circular faces as shown in Figure 3.12.b’s top right photo. When the sample was placed between pistons, this vug 
was at the top side, so it can be filled with water from the syringe. Nonetheless, the voltage amplitude was ruined at low 
compaction level. The bold data labels are the values that has been used to calculate the sonic velocities after subtracting 
corresponding lag times listed in Table 3.9. The first P-arrival is definitely at the first deflection from zero voltage line after 
the transmitted amplitude. The first shear arrival was selected at zero voltage line for an upward-crossing voltage peak, based 
on the margins of Isoframe modelled shear modulus values Ghs, so the measured shear value (Gs1 and Gs2) from equation (1.4) 
matches. Other adjacent shear first arrivals are also sort of probable first arrivals that introduce the uncertainty margins for the 
selected time value as discussed in Appendix C - Uncertainty in Shear First Arrival Picking-up.

Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
Now, after initial acceptance of S2-Wave to be the shear wave, the corresponding dependent calculated elastic 
moduli should be validated against the IF modelled values. Such validation is illustrated in Figure 4.16, Figure 
4.17 and Figure 4.18 for Vacuum Saturated Wet, Dry and Post Flooding Wet elastic moduli, respectively.  
For the compressional (P-Wave) modulus, the perfect match is inevitable, and the uncertainty margins are very 
limited as it comes only from the length as a measured dimension error. This is valid for all samples and saturation 
conditions. 
For the shear modulus and bulk moduli, the match with IF modelled values are not the best. However if the 
calculated IF fractions will be considered (Figure 4.19), for samples# 3 and 6, the pore vacuum saturated wet IF 
fraction was higher than the dry IF fraction, and the reverse for samples 2 and 4. The default is to have less IF in 
wet conditions than dry, because the saturation water should contribute to bear the stress. However higher IF 
fraction means more grain-to-grain contacts and not many grains in suspension. This makes it probable to have 
more tortuosity, as the grains are packed really tight and well cemented (∅ is already less the critical frequency 
that was assumed to be 100%). Therefore, the stabilization time (15-30 minutes) for the wet stress could be less 
than the required time to make even pore pressure gradient across the sample. This leads to induced pore pressure 
irregularity that gives a rise to stiffening effects.  
Comparing the same samples# 3 and 6 post flooding with the dry in terms of IF fractions, IF starts with higher 
values at the initial stress steps and then it experiences kind of inflection point in the middle of the loading way, 
and the IF wet becomes less than the dry. In other words, the load is carried in the beginning with higher 
framework contribution and then water starts to contribute later. 
For samples# 2 and 4, in both vacuum saturation and flooding, the wet IF fraction is less than the dry. Which is 
pretty normal.  
From Figure 4.20 the comparison between wet flooded and vacuum saturated samples shows relatively accepted 
match for samples# 2 and 4 in all elastic moduli. However, for sample# 6 the bulk modulus is higher in the 
vacuum saturated case, which is a combined effect of less shear and higher P (compressional) moduli. While 
sample# 3, the higher bulk modulus is rising due to only higher compressional modulus in the vacuum saturated 
case.

4.4 
Gassmann’s Substitution Validation 
 
Referring to Gassmann’s fluid substitution equation (1.8), the wet or saturated bulk modulus can be predicted 
from the dry bulk modulus value. Provided that all Gassmann’s assumptions are fulfilled, then the prediction 
should match the measured modulus. Dry measurements were carried out on dry samples, after being vacuum 
saturated and tested with water. So, the Gassmann’s predicted wet bulk moduli, will be derived from those dry 
measurements at each stress step and validated against the vacuum saturated wet measured bulk moduli. 
Using the same constituents’ (water and calcite) bulk moduli values listed in Table 3.10, the air in dry samples 
were substituted with water and predicted wet values were obtained as following: 
 
Figure 4.21: Validation of measured vacuum saturated wet bulk modulus against the Gassmann’s predicted wet modulus. The vertical and 
horizontal error bars are estimated by error propagation calculations (Appendix D - Error Propagation) to track the uncertainty in measured 
and Gassmann’s prediction calculations. 
From the showed results, the 4 tested samples are divided into two categories: 
- 
measured wet bulk modulus higher than the Gassmann’s predicted (underprediction), this is for samples# 
3 and 6 
- 
measured wet bulk modulus equal or less than Gassmann’s predicted (ideal/overprediction), and this is 
for samples# 2 and 4 
Such under and over/ideal prediction could happen when one or more of the Gassmann’s assumptions is not 
applied. But, because for all samples, it has been evenly assumed that the rock mineralogy is homogeneous 
(calcite and measured carbonate content ≈ 100% supports the assumption), the prediction deviation is probably 
owing to the other two assumptions: 
- 
either the pore volume statistical isotropy is violated, therefore there in-equilibrium in the temporary 
induced pore pressure gradient among pore networks (despite the tests were drained, it is probable to 
have uneven pressure distribution in the very tight tortuous pores)
