- 
or the propagated frequency of the ultrasonic wave was in the high range (higher than the characteristic 
Biot’s frequency for the samples with underpredicted bulk modulus), which could possibly lead to the 
first violation of the uneven pressure gradient through the out-of-phase water particles’ vibrations, that 
induce frictional forces along the water/solid interface inside the pores. 
To investigate for the possible right reason, Biot’s critical frequency and Pore Size Distribution of each sample 
are thoroughly analyzed as will follow. 
4.4.1 
Frequency Ratio 
Frequency ratio is the dimensionless ratio between the emitted frequency and the characteristic critical frequency 
for each core sample 
f
fc. The critical frequency fc is determined using equation (1.6). With well-defined pore fluid 
properties15 (Viscosity η and density ρf) and measured pore flow capacity parameters (porosity ∅ and liquid 
permeability k), the critical characteristic frequency will be obtained for this certain composite porous medium. 
Then from the definition of the viscous skin depth δ  given by equation (1.7), the frequency of the emitted wave 
is included in the equation. By dividing both equation (1.6) and (1.7), it will turn into:

(4.1) 
where k is in [mD] and δ is in [µm]. 
The permeability as a flow capacity indicator, is highly dependent on the porosity fraction. This correlation has 
been defined for homogenous porous network by Kozeny’s equation: 
 
k = c ∅3

The remaining variable to determine the Kozeny’s constant c, which has been investigated and defined by 
(Fabricius, Mortensen, & Engstrøm, 1998) for chalk in the Danish North Sea. The c constant was defined for a 
homogenous porosity model, where the fluid flow takes place according to Poiseuille’s law for flow through

As discussed earlier in the evaluation of the samples’ homogeneity and their NMR PSD above and below the 
average dominant pore radius (described in Figure 3.15), herein Kozeny’s permeability will be adopted to indicate 
the homogeneity of the sample, compared with the Klingenberg corrected permeability value that represent the 
entire bulk of the porous sample.  
Kozeny’s permeability calculated from equation (4.4), will be given as a permeability for a homogeneous little 
sample that weighs a couple of grams, which was tested in BET experiment to measure the monolayer covered 
pore surface area. The more homogenous the whole bulk sample is, the more matching between SBET for the 
couple of grams and the total pore surface area for the total sample’s mass. Therefore, the Kozeny’s permeability 
should approach the Klingenberg corrected permeability. This is illustrated in the following graph. 
 
Figure 4.22: Kozeny’s permeability vs. measured/calculated lab Klingenberg corrected permeability in [mD]. Kozeny’s value is defined 
based on the BET specific surface area, but in terms of the pore volume SP [µm-1]. The closer the cross points to the center diagonal line, 
the more homogenous the sample is. 
According to that, samples# 3 and 6 are more homogenous, which agrees with the same conclusion from the 
NMR PSD analysis based on the area under the curve. From here, another format or approach to assess the PSD 
homogeneity based on the permeabilities is to compare Biot’s critical frequency values measured from lab 
Klingenberg against the frequency from specific pore surface area SP. The results similarly showed more 
homogeneity in samples# 3 and 6, as in Figure 4.23.

Figure 4.23: A critical frequency convergence approach to assess sample’s homogeneity. Circular datapoints represent the critical 
frequency calculated from 𝑆𝐵𝐸𝑇 through equation (4.6), while triangular points are through the measured Klingenberg corrected

As shown in the previous figure, there is a horizontal line representing the emitted wave frequency. This has been 
determined manually from the recorded wet wave trains and confirmed by oscilloscope measure/curser functions 
to track the waveform in frequency units Figure 4.25. The frequency was manually picked up and calculated by 
measuring the time a well-defined (or half of) sine wave will take over the period time length of the scale. 
The wave of interest is the P-Wave rather than the shear. This is because the longitudinal P-Wave is the wave 
that travels in the fluid portion; therefore, this is the key wave’s frequency that defines the Biot’s frequency 
regime. The manually calculated frequency spectrum in wet samples are as shown in Figure 4.24. The final used 
frequency value was selected to be 210 [kHz], nonetheless there are less calculated dominant frequency of 170 
[kHz]. The 210 [kHz] frequency was the originally emitted frequency, which was confirmed by the stiffest 
metallic sample (Figure 4.25) and the oscilloscope. One of the stiffest core plugs (sample# 3), showed the same 
frequency value. Accordingly, it can be concluded that there is an associated frequency attenuation in softer 
samples, but no clues about where exactly along the sample length the attenuation prevails. So, the frequency of 
the emitted P-Waves is 210 [kHz].

through two different approaches, as an attempt to incorporate the homogeneity level in the context. First

frequency fc for each sample at each stress step based on the Klingenberg permeability through equation (1.6). In

porosity at each stress step which has been defined by equation (3.36).

From Figure 4.26, all samples showed low frequency regime where 
f
fc was always less than unity. The difference 
between the top and bottom graphs is the frequency ratio data point spreading over the X-axis, that addresses the 
homogeneity of the sample as explained in Figure 4.23. The closer the frequency ratios from top and bottom plots, 
the more homogenous the sample is.  
It is also interesting to compare such data points and frequency ratio, with some previous corresponding studies 
on limestones and calcites mineralogy. This will support also the credibility of the calculated data in this study. 
As discussed in (Fabricius, Bächle, & Eberli, Elastic moduli of dry and water-saturated carbonates-Effect of 
depositional texture, porosity, and permeability, 2010) similar plots to top graph of Figure 4.26 were created for 
larger number of the calcitic samples. By imposing current results over the corresponding plot from the previous 
study (figure 7.a), the results were localized in the low frequency regime area.  
 
Figure 4.27: Comparison between the current study results against previous studies on calcites. Frequency ratio is calculated from the 
direct Biot’s correlation using the lab measured permeability. Bulk modulus difference is the measured wet modulus minus the Gassmann’s 
predicted value by substituting water instead of air for a measured dry modulus. Larger datapoint size means higher hydrostatic stress. 
Therefore, all measured bulk moduli should match the Gassmann’s predictions at the corresponding stress level 
based on frequency ratios less than unity. However, samples# 3 and 6 and one last point of sample# 2 exceeded 
Gassmann’s predicted corresponding value. Sample# 4 may be considered relatively matching the predictions, 
with considering the uncertainty positive margins addressed in Figure 4.21.  
Based on that, there is a probability that pore size distribution of those violating samples contains higher fractions 
or contribution of characteristic pore radii that experience high frequency regime. So, if the critical frequency for 
each sample is translated into a characteristic threshold pore radius, then it would be possible to see how much of 
the PSD lies above and below this threshold radius i.e., if there is higher contribution from large pore radii that 
experience the high frequency frictional effect, this contribution will dominate the samples elastic properties. 
High frequency regimes are likely to happen in larger pores.

4.4.2 
Pore Size Distribution in terms of the Biot’s Critical radius and NMR Surface Relaxivity  
For this analysis, couple of correlation will be combined to find a direct correlation between the pore radius on 
the PSD and the characteristic Biot’s critical frequency for this size. Form the initial Biot’s critical frequency 
given by equation (1.6) in terms of the pore fluid properties (Viscosity η and density ρf) and measured pore flow 
capacity parameters (porosity ∅ and liquid permeability k).  Another Kozeny’s permeability is given by equation  
(4.4). Regardless of the homogeneity levels of the samples, Kozeny’s formula is directly proportional to the 
squared hydraulic radius of the porous medium. 
Now, using NMR correlations, a direct relation between average pore radius and the specific surface area per 
pore volume (hydraulic radius inverse) is required.  From the non-linear convergence NMR/MICP relation 
equation (3.16), the pore radius is defined by the T2 [ms] time according to convergence parameters C′ [µm/s] 
and n. Equation (3.16) can be rewritten as: 
 
T2 = ( r
C′)
n
 
(4.8) 
 
Substituting with this relation into T2 surface relaxation equation, the following relation will result: 
 
ρT2SP = (C′
r )
n
 
(4.9) 
 
 
SP =
(C′
r )
n
ρT2
⁄
 
(4.10) 
where SP is in [µm-1], C′ nonlinear convergence parameter implicitly addresses the surface relaxivity and pore 
shape factor [µm/s], r is the pore radius [µm] and  ρT2 is the NMR mineralogy surface relaxivity [µm/s]. 
Substituting with the value of SP from equation (4.10) into permeability equation (4.4), the permeability-pore 
radius relation becomes: 
 
k = c
∅ρT22
((C′
r )
n
)
2 
(4.11) 
where k is converted16 to [mD] using a conversion parameter 1 [mD] = 9.869×10-16 [m2]. 
From this equation, there are two permeability values in comparison, the lab measured Klingenberg permeability 
and the permeability from equation (4.11). Both will result in a critical frequency value, but all other variables 
are the same to calculate the two values of critical frequency. Thus, the critical frequency comparison reduces 
into the permeability comparison. Therefore, if the pore radius value is obtained for make calculated permeability 
matches the measured value, this pore size will be the same pore size that matches critical frequencies. This is the 
characteristic threshold or critical pore radius, then. 
To complete the calculations, the surface relaxivity is needed. It could be directly calculated from the C′ 
convergence parameter, but with assumption of a shape factor17 value FS . But to minimize the number of 
assumptions, a more generalized approach is adopted based on NMR and MICP PSD. 
 
 
 
16 With dimensional analysis for this equation, the variable exponent n will be problematic. However, the values for this 
exponent as listed in Table 3.5 ranges from 0.9-1.1, this can be assumed to be 1, for the sake of dimensional consistence. 
17 The shape factor is mathematically obtained to be 2 or 3 for cylindrical or spherical pores.

Pore Surface Relaxivity from NMR PSD

al., 2020)through the following equation: 
 
T2lm = (ρT2SP)−1

From the surface relaxivity calculations, it is obvious that the approximate match between NMR and 
MICP is only for samples# 2 and 6. Referring to the vacuum saturated PSD plots in Figure 3.13, it is 
indicated that samples# 2 and 6 have a more localized pore size distribution, while the PSD banks (higher 
and lower pore radii ranges)  showed relatively low frequency compared to samples # 3 and 4, for which 
the surface relaxivity  ρT2MICP was significantly biased by those extreme banks. Also, the calculated 
average surface relaxivity could be compared to titration carbonate content to see if there is any 
sensitivity. This is plotted in Figure 4.28, where there is a sort of inverse relation between the carbonate 
content and surface relaxivity. The more the non-calcite impurities are in the mineralogy, the higher the 
ability of the pore grain walls to relax hydrogen proton (shorter relaxation time).

under the curve19 above/below those calculated characteristic pore radii. The results, as simulated in Figure 4.30, 
depict the following: 
- 
Vacuum Saturated samples# 3 and 6, almost 100% of the pores in the sample are larger than characteristic 
pore radius. This means, those two samples are significantly sensitive to the frequency and are very likely 
to undergo the high frequency stiffening effects due to the frictional forces at the pore fluid/grain 
interface. Moreover, with the low Klingenberg permeability for those samples indicates poor pore 
connectivity especially sample# 6 (not pore tightness, because all samples almost have the same pore 
tightness range, but with quite variant distributions), this lack of connectivity also make it very likely to 
violate the Gassmann’s Substitution correlation assumption of even pore pressure gradient distribution. 
This could increase the possibility for high frequency effects. 
- 
Vacuum Saturated sample# 2, 62% of the PSD is above the characteristic pore radius, which means the 
probability to experience the high frequency stiffening effects is found and distributed over 62% of the 
total number of the pores. 
- 
Vacuum Saturated and Post Flooded sample# 4, almost 100% of the pores are smaller than the 
corresponding critical pore radius. Therefore, probability for high frequency effects is infinitesimal. 
However, for the vacuum saturated sample there is some doubts about the Klingenberg corrected 
permeability as explained earlier in Figure 3.3. The uncertainty in the liquid permeability value comes 
from the probable gas bypassing flow around the core sample inside the porosimeter core holder through 
the large surface vugs. No matter what the mean pressure value is applied, the gas permeability was 
always high with a statistical variance value of 13 mD from 5 measurements. This is why the calculated 
critical frequency and corresponding characteristic pore radius are probably uncertain.

this is because the saturation levels achieved were not too far from 100%, as conveyed from Figure 3.11.

Figure 4.30:The numerical simulation for the volumetric fractions above/below the Biot’s Characteristic Pore radius. Black dots represent 
the grains and the wider they are separated, the larger the pore radius among them. 
Based on that, particularly for sample# 4 with the doubtful Klingenberg permeability and characteristic pore 
radius, a pore radius sensitivity was investigated to get a better understanding for this sample’s behavior. The 
sensitivity as listed and summarized in Table 4.3 and Figure 4.31, was done on three radius values. 
- 
1st radius is the characteristic pore radius calculated from the measured Klingenberg permeability (from 
Table 4.2). 
- 
2nd pore radius is the most MICP dominant radius (Table 3.5). 
- 
3rd pore radius is the harmonic average of the MICP dominant radii (Table 3.5), that was a better match 
with the central NMR logarithmic mean radius (equation (3.33) and Figure 3.14) that was also well 
representing the entire bulk sample and NMR was not biased by the external vugs, likewise the 
permeability. 
The plotted results compared to the emitted frequency of ultrasonic waves tell: 
==PAGE:82==
 
 
74 
Characterization of Biot’s Critical Frequency in Core Plugs 
- 
sample# 4 is very likely to have higher critical frequency far from the emitted wave frequency, based on 
a smaller actual dominant radius. Therefore, again the possibility to undergo high frequency stiffening 
effect is extremely low. 
- 
sample# 2, there is a fairly good convergence between all assumed dominant characteristic pore radii and 
the critical frequencies. However, all critical frequencies are higher than the emitted frequency. Although, 
the possibility for finding larger pore size with larger permeability and lower critical frequency will move 
up from 62% (for the Klingenberg characteristic radius) to 79% (Figure 3.15 corresponds the average 
dominant pore radius), the sample’s dominant characteristic radius and critical frequency is still too high 
to have frictional stiffening effects, but it is not impossible to have it due to the large probability with 
high number of pores. 
- 
samples# 3 and 6 the avg. dominant larger radius will impose lower critical frequencies closer to the 
emitted value, compared with the other samples# 2 and 4. The probability for high frequency effects in 
large pores, will drop down from being found in~ 100% of the pores (Figure 4.30) to be found in 68 and 
39% of the pores (Figure 3.15) for samples# 3 and 6, respectively.

5 
Conclusions 
 
According to Gassmann’s fluid substitution, the 2 key assumptions for a viable predicted substitution in a 
homogenous rock mineralogy are:  
- 
pore volume statistical isotropy in terms of the pore pressure gradient and the pore network is connected  
- 
and the non-existence of any high frequency effects that will induce irregular pore pressure gradients and 
stiffens the sample (under-prediction). 
After studying mechanical/acoustic behavior of the 4 subject samples and the petrophysical characterization, the 
water saturated (wet) bulk modulus was found higher and underpredicted by the Gassmann’s correlation (equation 
(1.8)) in samples# 3 and 6 (the most homogenous one according to Kozeny’s validity and PSD analysis). Whereas 
for samples# 2 and 4 most of the sample data points are more or less the same (slightly overpredicted by 
Gassmann’s) as the predicted values. All is conveyed in Figure 4.21. 
Based on the ratio between the hydraulic radius SP
−1 [µm] (equation (4.3)) and the viscous skin depth δ [µm] 
((1.7)), and the calculated frequency ratio f fc
⁄  (either from BET or porosity/permeability ratio), all results showed 
that all samples are in the low frequency regime, and they should comply with the Gassamann’s assumptions.  
However, from the pore size distribution and the attempt to estimate a characteristic pore radius (equation(4.11)) 
that corresponds to Biot’s critical frequency fc, the results could probably justify the Gassmann’s substitution 
violation: 
- 
Sample# 2:  there is 62% of the pores above the characteristic pore radius. Therefore, there is probability 
to experience high frequency effects at those larger pores whose critical threshold frequency is smaller, 
which is the case with one point at the highest stress is above the ideal match line (Figure 4.21). But, 
from the Isoframe effective model and the corresponding IF fraction representing the solid grains’ 
contribution in the framework, IF for a vacuum saturated sample was less than the dry one (Figure 4.19), 
this means the water contributes to carrying the load and there are good grain-to-fluid contacts, which is 
likely translated into a better pore flow capacity and less touristy (supported by a 2nd highest Klingenberg 
permeability of 40 mD). Therefore, if there are some large pores undergoing the high frequency 
inertial/frictional forces, the good pore communication would obscure them. 
 
- 
Sample# 4: 100% of the pores are less than the calculated large characteristic pore radius. The probability 
for high frequency effects was high with a very low fc value, which is strongly uncertain, because the 
uncertainty of Klingenberg permeability that corresponds to that characteristic pore radius. It was too 
large, compared with more reasonable average dominant pore radius from NMR and MICP, that gave 
much higher critical frequency (Figure 4.31) as well, that makes it less probable to yield such violating 
high frequency effects along the applied frequency of 210 kHz. The same scenario with IF and model 
and pore communication for sample# 2 applies here. 
 
- 
Sample# 3 and 6: around 100% of the pores are higher than the characteristic pore radius.  The 
probability for high frequency experience could apply for some pores, even if the emitted frequency is 
less than fc. In such high population with large pore sizes that imply less Biot’s critical frequency, it is 
very likely to encounter high frequency effects in those dominant pores that will affect the entire sample. 
Form IF values, higher IF in wet conditions, could be reasoned for the high grain-to-grain contact and 
poor communication between pores (which violates Gassmann’s assumptions), that will make the high 
frequency inertial effects more probable due to uneven pore pressure distribution. 
Overall, the out-of-phase inertial forces under applied high frequency ratio (frequencies above fc ) is 
considerably affecting the porous rock mechanical behavior. Such induced out-of-phase relative fluid/solid 
oscillating motion and pore pressure gradients could be exploited in pore fluid drainage, as a result of high 
frequency effects. However, this could be thoroughly investigated with different pore fluids and bigger 
population of rock cores and lithologies.

A 
Appendix A - NMR and MICP PSD Analysis and Convergence Calculations 
 
NMR pore size distribution is measured in the lab in units of T2 [ms] versus fractional porosity (Figure 
A.1 and Figure A.2 1st rows). Fractional porosity ratio to the total cumulative NMR porosity (sum of 
all porosity fractions) represents the pore fluid saturation (Sw) in that pore size range (Figure A.1 and 
Figure A.2 2nd rows). The NMR signal is affected by the saturation fluid in the pore volume. Tape water 
is used, and water is assumed to be a wetting phase.  
On the other hand, the MICP PTSD is experimentally determined by mercury injection (Hg is always a 
non-wetting phase) with certain volumes at monitored pressures that can translated into pore (also 
means pore throat in some literatures) radius r [µm] (Figure A.1 and Figure A.2 1st rows). After the end 
of experiment, the total injected volume [cc] is known and fractional step intruded volume of mercury 
was monitored [cc/gm] for a certain sample weight [gm], therefore the ratio between them will define 
the Hg saturation SHg (Figure A.1 and Figure A.2 2nd rows).  
Injected mercury as a non-wetting phase could be imagined as a displacing fluid that removed the 
wetting phase from the pores, provided that the pores are only saturated either with the wetting or the 
non-wetting phase. Therefore, this is translated into: 
 
SHg = 1 −Sw 
(A.1) 
 
Then from the calculated NMR Sw, the imaginary corresponding SHg is calculated from equation (A.1). 
Then, in Excel20 the closest corresponding pore radius from MICP will be picked up according to the 
closest match between the SHgNMR = 1 −Sw from NMR and the SHg from MICP (Figure A.1 and 
Figure A.2 3rd rows).  
Now, there are one measured matching pore radius from MICP and another calculated pore radius from 
NMR data through the non-linear equation (3.16). Through this equation with an assumed initial value 
for C′ and n, there will be a calculated pore radius rT2 [µm] vector that should fit the matching MICP 
pore radius rMICP vector. The best convergence or fitting between both radii vectors (i=1: end) is done 
by the least squared residual method according to the following equation: 
 
∑(ln rT2i −ln rMICPi)
2
end
i=1
= ∑(ln C′T2i
1
n −ln rMICPi)
2
end
i=1
 
(A.2) 
 
The non-linear convergence is achieved using Excel Solver add-in by finding the convergence 
parameters values (C′ and n) that set the least squared residuals summation to a minimum (Figure A.1 
and Figure A.2 4th rows). The convergence parameters are listed earlier in Table 3.5 for 4 vacuum 
saturated samples and in Table 3.6 for sample# 4 post flooding. 
 
 
20 Built-in Excel functions were used to find the closest match of rMICPi from T2 according to the SHg and SHgNMR 
vectors. The script was:  
rMICPi = INDEX (rMICP1: rMICPend, XMATCH (MIN (ABS (SHg1: SHgend −SHgNMRi)) , ABS (SHgi=1
end −SHgNMRi) , 0,1)) 
0 means exact match, and 1 to specify the vector’s search mode from first (i = 1) to last (i = end).0

B 
Appendix B - Travel Time Calibration (Circuit Lag Time) 
 
As listed in Table 3.9, there are three lag time values correspond to each type of the three wave phases. 
The lag time ∆t is the zero-length travel time the ultrasonic wave takes through the circuit itself and 
pistons. The zero-length must be obtained and corrected for before evaluating the non-zero length travel 
time in core plugs, therefore lag time is subtracted from each phase first arrival correspondingly, and 
the result is the net travel time of the wave phase in the sample. This correction value is determined by 
using different length of single material (Standard Test Method for Laboratory Determination of Pulse 
Velocities and Ultrasonic Elastic Constants of Rock) and by plotting the picked-up first arrival of the 
wave phases versus the variant length of each sample, the lag time will be the Y-intercept at zero length. 
In this study, the 3 different calibration metallic pellets (shown in Figure B.1) were used and the mean 
of all zero-length times for each wave phase was the overall lag time value 
 
Figure B.1: The metallic calibration pellet sets used for obtained the circuit lag time. The pellets were numbered 1, 2 and 3 
corresponding to the left, middle and right sets. 
The first wave phase arrivals were picked up as the first deflection or voltage deviation from the zero-
voltage line for P-wave and as the first significant voltage deflection’s intersection with the zero-voltage 
line. Shear wave first arrival was not that obscured with other converted P-waves or reflections due to 
the metallic homogeneity and stiffness. 
The first arrival results are collected in Figure B.2 and the final intercept corresponding to the ultimate 
circuit lag time is as listed below as a mean value for all intercepts for the single wave phase: 
Lag Time (Δtp)  
[μs] 
Lag Time (Δts1) 
 [μs] 
Lag Time (Δts2)  
[μs] 
2.85 
7.27 
6.58 
 
The shear wave first arrivals were found to be clear at early first significant up crossing voltage peaks, 
and based on this pattern of the picking up, this methodology with consistently generalized to the shear 
wave pick up on the tested core plugs.

C 
Appendix C - Uncertainty in Shear First Arrival Picking-up 
 
Due to the inevitable possibility for the faster P-Wave conversion to secondary shear waves, in addition 
to multiple wave reflections at different interfaces, the slower primary transmitted shear wave is likely 
obscured with all those induced secondary-originated waves and the shear piezoelectric crystals will 
vibrate and respond to all what they receive. Therefore, the picked-up shear wave first arrivals were 
challenging to be precisely spotted, and this introduces relative uncertainty and error margins in the 
time values selected for further calculations, e.g., shear velocity and dependent elastic moduli.  
Unlike the shear wave, the P-Wave is the fastest, therefore the first early arrival is significantly clear 
and comes early enough before any secondary slower multiples. 
For instance, for the vacuum saturated wet sample# 2 at the third stress level (Figure C.1.c), the recorded 
S1-Wave showed three possible values for the first shear wave arrival. The selected value on which 
calculations was based on the time value in bold (46.775 µ.seconds). However, this arrival time could 
be questioned and be changed to a later time of 46.9 µ.seconds, at which the significant voltage 
deflection was up crossing the zero-voltage line. On the other hand, the earlier arrival time value of 
46.275 could be a possible arrival and the voltage amplitude was supposed to complete crossing up 
towards the peak of the amplitude, but some other secondary interference was introduced, and the peak 
was completely attenuated and went flat for a while.  
The same methodology was introduced for all wave to track any possible uncertainty in the shear first 
arrivals. Some arrivals were very clear and not much interference or uncertainty. Some other arrivals 
were not found exactly on the zero-voltage line intercept, therefore, there is 2 uncertain arrival time 
values at the first before (negative voltage amplitude side) and after (positive voltage amplitude side) 
recorded points below and above zero-voltage line. 
All time arrivals are listed in Table D.3, Table D.7 and Table D.9 for the vacuum saturated wet, dry and 
post flooding wet samples, respectively.

D 
Appendix D - Error Propagation 
 
Due to uncertainties in lab measurements due to irregularities or human error, the error in an 
independent measurement propagates through any dependent variable. Therefore, it is desirable to show 
some confidence margins for the results. The measurement errors are random and independent of each 
other. And for the scope of this study, the independent error sources are mainly originating from:  
- 
Samples’ dimensions: length and diameter that were measured three times each per sample at 
different spots on the sample. 
- 
Bulk mass: For the wet mass of water saturated samples, the fluctuation in values is probably 
due to slight reduction of water saturation, where water might scape slightly from the parafilm. 
- 
Shear wave first arrival time picking up: this is mainly due to the challenge of obtaining a 
pure shear wave train, without any inevitable interference/overlapping with the faster P-wave 
and reflections or noises. 
- 
Pore volume uncertainty, that is obtained from the porosimeter calibration, that reflects in the 
accuracy of measured porosity value. 
The error or the uncertainty in independent measured variables, is given by the standard deviation 
(square root of variance, σ) of the measurements’ population sample. The variance of a calculated 
variable can be approximated by a partial derivative form that quantify the propagated error magnitude. 
For instance, y=f(x,z) is a function in x,z. Then the variance of the product function y is given by: 
 
σy2 ≈σx2 (δy
δx)
2
+ σz2 (δy
δz)
2
 
(D.1) 
  
For the scope of the measurement uncertainties in this study are: 
- 
Sample cross section area 
- 
Bulk volume of a cylinder 
- 
Bulk density 
- 
Sonic velocity 
- 
P and shear moduli 
- 
Bulk modulus 
- 
Gas porosity
